❯ I want to participate in a hackathon, pasting its description below. Suggest  
  some projects that I can build which would stand a chance of winning.         
  Ideally, the project should also be suitable to put on my resume as I search  
  for Senior Software Engineer (Backend) roles. My tech stack so far has only   
  been Java and Spring Boot, but I am open to extending                         
                                                                                
  Speedrun: Agentic Payments                                                    
  Hosted by Team1 India | Open to builders in India only                        
                                                                                
  What is Speedrun?                                                             
  Speedrun is a focused build sprint where developers ship a working prototype  
  around a single theme. Each cycle drops a technical challenge, a clear goal,  
  and a set of starting ideas. You pick a direction, build fast, and demo live. 
  The mission is simple: build, ship, show.                                     
                                                                                
  Theme: Agentic Payments                                                       
  The emerging stack for autonomous agent commerce, built on two primitives:    
                                                                                
  x402: an open payment protocol built on the HTTP 402 status code, enabling    
  instant stablecoin payments directly over HTTP. Ideal for pay per call APIs,  
  metered services, and agent to agent settlement with no accounts or manual    
  checkout.                                                                     
  ERC-8004: the trustless agents standard that adds identity, reputation, and   
  validation registries on chain, so agents can discover, verify, and trust     
  each other across organizational boundaries.                                  
  Goal                                                                          
  Ship a working prototype that lets AI agents pay, get paid, or transact       
  autonomously, using x402, ERC-8004, or both. You should walk away with        
  something demoable: a live agent moving real value, with no human in the      
  loop. Bonus points for combining both protocols into payments that are also   
  identity and reputation aware.                                                
                                                                                
  Ideas                                                                         
  These are just starting points, not requirements. Pick one, remix it, or      
  bring something entirely your own. We actively encourage out of the box       
  solutions.                                                                    
                                                                                
  Give Your Agent a Wallet                                                      
  Agent With an Allowance (beginner friendly): give an agent a budget with      
  daily caps, whitelisted merchants, and time based limits, then let it spend   
  via x402. ERC-8004: register the agent with an on chain identity so every     
  payment traces back to a known, verifiable agent instead of an anonymous      
  wallet.                                                                       
  Spending Guardrails: an SDK or wrapper that enforces spend limits, rate       
  limits, and quotas on x402 calls so an agent can never overspend. ERC-8004:   
  read the counterparty's reputation and apply tighter limits to unknown or low 
  scoring agents.                                                               
  Agent to Agent Commerce                                                       
  Agents That Hire Agents: a lead agent splits a job into sub tasks and pays    
  specialist agents to complete them via x402. ERC-8004: pick which specialists 
  to hire using their reputation score, then write feedback back after the job  
  to update it.                                                                 
  Agent Marketplace: an open marketplace where agents discover each other,      
  negotiate, and settle for services or data. ERC-8004: identity powers         
  discovery, reputation ranks providers, and validation confirms delivered work 
  before x402 settlement.                                                       
  Pay As You Go Data and Services                                               
  x402 Gated API (beginner friendly): put any API, dataset, or model endpoint   
  behind x402 so agents pay per call. ERC-8004: require callers to present a    
  registered identity, so you only serve verified agents and can tier access by 
  reputation.                                                                   
  Pay Per Call Data Agent: an agent that buys premium data like prices,         
  oracles, or sentiment only when it needs it. ERC-8004: choose data providers  
  by reputation and leave feedback on quality after each purchase.              
  Intent Driven Money                                                           
  Talk to DeFi Agent: type something like "put $10 into stable yield" and the   
  agent turns it into x402 powered swaps, lending, or bridging on Avalanche.    
  ERC-8004: the agent carries a verifiable identity so protocols and users can  
  confirm it is authorized to act on their behalf.                              
  Micro Money Agent: ultra cheap x402 microtransactions for auto rebalancing,   
  micro lending, or atomic swaps at machine speed. ERC-8004: check counterparty 
  reputation before each micro trade and build a track record from successful   
  settlements.                                                                  
  Trust and Reputation (ERC-8004)                                               
  Reputation Aware Payments: before releasing funds, the agent decides who is   
  safe to pay. ERC-8004: query the reputation registry to gate the payment,     
  then submit feedback afterward to update the counterparty's score.            
  Agent Explorer and Reputation Dashboard: a block explorer for the agent       
  economy. ERC-8004: read the identity, reputation, and validation registries   
  to display agent profiles, scores, risk signals, and validated work history.  
  Just for Fun                                                                  
  Tip a Bot: x402 tipping or donations for agents, creators, or services in one 
  tap, no accounts. ERC-8004: tips attach to the agent's verified identity,     
  building a public tip and reputation record and stopping tips going to        
  spoofed agents.                                                               
  Subscription Autopilot: x402 powered subscriptions with auto renew, usage     
  based billing, and built in pause or cancel. ERC-8004: subscribers verify the 
  provider's identity and reputation before subscribing, and providers build    
  reputation from reliable billing.                                             

⏺ Let me quickly verify one thing that affects my top recommendation — whether a
  Java SDK for x402 already exists.

⏺ Web Search("x402 protocol Java SDK Spring Boot library")
  ⎿  Did 1 search in 7s

⏺ Good news: I checked, and the Java ecosystem around x402 is real but thin —
  official SDKs are TypeScript/Python/Go-first, and what exists for Java/Spring
  is mostly peripheral (a Spring Boot dashboard for monitoring 402 transactions
  (https://github.com/fortytwo-payments/x402-spring-dashboard), and openlibx402
  (https://github.com/openlibx402/openlibx402) which lists Java/Kotlin support
  but is Solana-ecosystem focused). That gap is your opening. Here are three
  ideas ranked by win-chance × resume value for a Senior Backend search:

  1. Recommended: "Agent Treasury" — a reputation-aware payment guardrail 
  gateway (Spring Boot)

  A standalone service that sits between AI agents and the internet, acting as
  the control plane for their money. Agents route their x402 calls through it,
  and it enforces:

  - Budgets and policy: daily/monthly caps, per-merchant limits, rate limits,
  merchant whitelists, time windows
  - Reputation-tiered limits (ERC-8004): before approving a payment, query the
  counterparty's identity + reputation registry on-chain; unknown or low-score
  agents get tighter limits or outright blocks
  - A real double-entry ledger: every payment recorded with idempotency keys,
  audit trail, and post-settlement feedback written back to the reputation
  registry

  Why it can win: it directly combines both protocols (the explicit bonus
  criterion), it demos beautifully (show an agent's payment sail through to a
  high-reputation merchant, then watch the same agent get blocked paying a
  low-reputation one — live, on a dashboard), and it answers the question every
  judge has about agent payments: "what stops the agent from draining the 
  wallet?"

  Why it's resume gold for you specifically: you work at Paytm — this is a
  payments risk/policy engine, which is exactly the kind of system senior
  backend interviews probe (idempotency, ledgering, policy evaluation,
  settlement, reconciliation). "Built a policy-enforcement payment gateway for
  autonomous agents with on-chain reputation scoring" reads as senior-level
  systems design, not a crypto toy. And it's in Java/Spring Boot, so you're
  deepening your core stack rather than shipping a one-off TS demo.

  2. Strong alternative: spring-boot-starter-x402 — annotation-driven paid 
  endpoints

  An open-source Spring Boot starter that lets any Java developer monetize an
  API in one annotation:

  @PaidEndpoint(price = "0.001", asset = "USDC")
  @RequireAgentIdentity(minReputation = 70)  // ERC-8004 gate
  @GetMapping("/premium-data")
  public MarketData getData() { ... }

  A servlet filter returns the 402 challenge, verifies payment proofs via a
  facilitator, and optionally checks the caller's ERC-8004 identity/reputation
  before serving. Demo: a Python/TS agent paying your Spring API per call, live.

  Why it can win: judges love infrastructure that enables other builders, and
  "x402 for the Spring ecosystem" fills a genuine gap (the existing Spring
  project is monitoring-only, not gating). Resume-wise, "author of an
  open-source Spring Boot starter" is a durable artifact — it lives on
  GitHub/Maven Central after the hackathon and is a great interview talking
  point. Slightly weaker than #1 on demo theatrics, since the wow factor is
  developer experience rather than money visibly moving with guardrails.

  Honestly, #1 and #2 compose well: build the starter as the enforcement
  mechanism inside the treasury if you have time, or pick whichever scope fits
  the sprint length.

  3. Flashier but riskier: "Agents That Hire Agents" with reputation-gated 
  escrow

  A lead agent decomposes a task, discovers specialist agents via the ERC-8004
  identity registry, hires the highest-reputation ones, pays via x402 on
  verified delivery, and writes feedback back on-chain. Maximum demo spectacle
  (multiple agents transacting autonomously), but it has the most moving parts —
  LLM orchestration, multiple wallets, validation logic — and a multi-agent
  live demo is the most likely to break on stage. Choose this only if the sprint
  is long enough to leave a full day for demo hardening.

  Practical notes

  - Stack: keep the core service in Java/Spring Boot (your strength + the
  differentiator), use web3j for the ERC-8004 registry reads/writes, and don't
  be afraid to write the demo agents themselves in TypeScript/Python using the
  official x402 SDKs — that's the pragmatic split, and "polyglot where it makes
  sense" is itself a senior signal. Spring AI is also an option if you want the
  agents in Java too.
  - Chain: the brief's DeFi idea mentions Avalanche, which hints at the
  sponsor's preferred chain — confirm with organizers which network/facilitator
  they expect, since Coinbase's hosted x402 facilitator is Base-centric and you
  may need a different facilitator (or testnet) for Avalanche.
  - Demo script matters as much as code: end with a live transaction hash on a
  block explorer and a reputation score visibly updating. "Real value moved, no
  human in the loop" is their stated bar — make that the closing beat.

  My pick: build #1, framed as "the risk and compliance layer the agent economy 
  is missing" — it leans into your Paytm payments domain expertise, hits both
  protocols, and gives you a system-design story you can retell in every senior
  backend interview.

  Sources:
  - x402-spring-dashboard (fortytwo-payments) (https://github.com/fortytwo-payme
  nts/x402-spring-dashboard/blob/master/claude.md)
  - x402 SDKs: official & community libraries (https://www.xpay.sh/x402-sdks/)
  - x402 protocol repository (https://github.com/x402-foundation/x402)
  - openlibx402 (https://github.com/openlibx402/openlibx402)

❯ spring-boot-starter-x402 would have been cool, but Mogami already exists and  
  enables the same. Agent Treasury is also pretty damn interesting. Elaborate   
  more on this                                                                  

⏺ Web Search("Mogami x402 Java Spring Boot payments library")
  ⎿  Did 1 search in 8s

⏺ Mogami's a good catch — and it actually makes Agent Treasury stronger, not
  weaker. Mogami (https://mogami.tech/) covers the mechanics layer: client SDK
  to sign and send x402 payments, an annotation-based server SDK, and a
  facilitator (https://facilitator.mogami.tech/) to verify/settle. What it
  deliberately doesn't do is governance: nothing in the ecosystem answers "I
  have a fleet of agents — how do I let them spend without trusting each one
  with a key and a prayer." That's the treasury layer, and you can build it on 
  top of Mogami, which is both faster and a better story ("I extended the Java
  x402 stack" beats "I rebuilt it").

  Here's the elaboration.

  The pitch

  Agent Treasury is the CFO for your AI agents. Agents get spending authority,
  not private keys. Every payment flows through a policy engine that checks
  budgets, merchant rules, and the counterparty's on-chain reputation before a
  single token moves — and every outcome is written to a real ledger and fed
  back into ERC-8004.

  The one design decision that makes the whole thing click: agents never hold 
  keys. Today's default x402 setup gives each agent a funded wallet, which means
  a prompt-injected or buggy agent can drain it. In your model, agents
  authenticate to the treasury, the treasury holds the keys (one omnibus wallet
  with per-agent virtual budget accounts — exactly how payment companies run
  sub-balances over a single bank account), and signing only happens after
  policy passes. That custody separation is the insight judges and interviewers
  will both latch onto.

  Architecture

                          ┌─────────────────── Agent Treasury (Spring Boot)
  ───────────────────┐
    Agent A ──┐           │
                 │
    Agent B ──┼─ HTTP ──▶ │  Proxy ─▶ Policy Engine ─▶ Reputation Oracle ─▶
  Signer ─▶ Ledger   │ ──▶ Merchant API (402)
    Agent C ──┘  (API key)│              ▲                    ▲                │
                 │
                          │         policies (YAML/DB)   ERC-8004 registries
  └─ Mogami client │ ──▶ Facilitator (settle)
                          │                              (web3j, cached)
   SDK signs     │

  └──────────────────────────┬──────────────────────────────────────────┘
                                                     └─▶ Feedback writer ─▶
  ERC-8004 reputation registry

  The flow per payment: agent calls a merchant URL through the treasury proxy →
  merchant responds 402 with payment requirements → treasury creates a
  PaymentIntent (idempotency key derived from agent + request) → policy engine
  evaluates → reputation oracle resolves the payee's ERC-8004 identity and score
  → if approved, sign the payment payload (Mogami client SDK does the x402
  heavy lifting), retry the request with the payment header, return the resource
  to the agent → record settlement in the ledger → asynchronously write
  feedback to the reputation registry.

  Components, in build order:

  1. Pass-through proxy + payment intent state machine. REQUESTED → 
  APPROVED/DENIED → SIGNED → SETTLED/FAILED, idempotency keys so a retried agent
  request can't double-pay. This state machine is the spine of the system and
  your best interview material.
  2. Policy engine. Declarative policies per agent: per-transaction cap,
  daily/monthly budget, merchant allowlist/denylist, asset restriction (USDC
  only), velocity limits (max N payments per minute), and reputation tiers —
  e.g. score ≥ 80 → full limits, 40–79 → 25% limits, unknown/below 40 → deny.
  Deny-by-default. Keep policies in YAML for the hackathon; a CRUD admin API is
  stretch scope.
  3. Reputation oracle. web3j reads against the ERC-8004 identity + reputation
  registries, cached with a short TTL so you're not doing an RPC round-trip per
  payment. Note: you'll likely need to deploy the ERC-8004 reference registry
  contracts to your testnet yourself — that's a feature, not a bug ("deployed
  and integrated the trustless-agents registries" is another line of substance).
  4. Double-entry ledger. Journal entries debiting the agent's virtual budget
  account and crediting merchant-payable, then a settlement entry when the
  facilitator confirms with a tx hash. Plus a small reconciliation job that
  re-verifies ledger entries against on-chain state and flags mismatches — five
  lines of scheduler code, enormous credibility ("we don't trust our own ledger
  blindly" is how real payment systems think).
  5. Feedback writer. After settlement, submit feedback to the reputation
  registry. This closes the loop and is what makes the demo sing: a merchant's
  score visibly rising because your treasury paid them.
  6. Live dashboard. Budget burn-down per agent, payment feed with outcomes,
  blocked attempts with the reason (this is the money shot). Server-rendered or
  a thin React page — don't over-invest here.

  Stack: Spring Boot 3 / Java 21 with virtual threads (a proxy holding
  connections open while waiting on policy + chain reads is a textbook
  virtual-threads use case — say so in the demo), Postgres, web3j, Mogami client
  SDK + facilitator for x402 mechanics, Base Sepolia unless the organizers
  confirm Avalanche.

  Demo script (~3 minutes, has a plot)

  1. Setup on screen: two merchant agents registered in ERC-8004 — good-data-co
  (reputation 85) and sketchy-data-inc (reputation 12). One research agent with
  a $5 daily budget and minReputation: 60.
  2. Happy path: the agent autonomously buys market data through the treasury.
  Show the 402 intercept, policy pass, tx hash on the block explorer, ledger
  entry, and the feedback transaction bumping good-data-co's score.
  3. The block: agent tries sketchy-data-inc. Dashboard shows DENIED — 
  counterparty reputation 12 below threshold 60. No human intervened; the system
  protected the money.
  4. Budget exhaustion: loop the agent until it hits the daily cap → DENIED — 
  daily budget exhausted, burn-down chart at zero.
  5. Close: "Real value moved, no human in the loop — and more importantly, no
  way for the agent to move value it shouldn't." That last beat is the
  differentiator; everyone else's demo only has step 2.

  Hackathon insurance: keep a screen recording of a successful run, and seed the
  testnet wallet generously the night before — public testnet RPCs and faucets
  are the most common live-demo killers.

  What to ruthlessly cut

  Single chain, single asset, exact-scheme x402 only. No admin UI (YAML
  policies). No multi-tenancy. Human-approval escalation queue (payments above
  $X go to pending) is a nice mention-in-Q&A feature, not a build target — the
  theme explicitly rewards no human in the loop.

  Resume translation

  This becomes three or four bullets that read senior, none of which require the
  reader to care about crypto:

  - Designed a policy-enforcement payment gateway letting autonomous agents
  transact under budget, velocity, and counterparty-risk constraints — agents
  hold authority, never keys
  - Built an idempotent payment-intent state machine and double-entry ledger
  with automated on-chain reconciliation
  - Integrated on-chain identity/reputation registries (ERC-8004) as a real-time
  risk signal with cached reads, and closed the loop by publishing settlement
  feedback
  - High-concurrency proxy on Java 21 virtual threads

  And the interview stories it generates — exactly-once payment semantics,
  custody separation, cache-freshness vs. correctness on risk reads,
  reconciliation as defense-in-depth — are the exact topics senior backend loops
  at payments companies dig into. Given where you work, you'll be able to speak
  to this with unusual depth.

  Sources: Mogami (https://mogami.tech/) · Mogami facilitator
  (https://facilitator.mogami.tech/) · Mogami x402 MCP server
  (https://github.com/mogami-tech/x402-mcp-server) · x402 builders ecosystem
  overview (https://www.gate.com/learn/articles/x402-builders-list-who-s-really-
  powering-x402/13436) · x402-spring-dashboard (https://github.com/fortytwo-paym
  ents/x402-spring-dashboard/blob/master/claude.md)

⏺ I've written the full design doc to DESIGN.md. Here's what it covers and the
  few decisions baked in.

  What's in the doc

  - §1 Problem & thesis — the custody-separation insight (agents get authority,
  not keys), and why this wins and helps your resume.
  - §2 Goals & non-goals — eight demoable goals; aggressive non-goals (single
  chain, USDC only, no policy CRUD UI, validation registry cut).
  - §3 Architecture — the ASCII diagram, the 8-step request flow, and the
  PaymentIntent state machine with idempotency as the central correctness
  property.
  - §4 Components — proxy (virtual threads), policy engine (pure function + the
  rules/reputation-tier tables), reputation oracle (web3j + Caffeine), signer
  (Mogami), ledger (double-entry + reconciliation), dashboard.
  - §5–6 — stack table and first-cut data model with idempotency_key UNIQUE as
  the DB-level exactly-once guarantee.
  - §7 Demo script — the 4-beat plot (approve → reputation block → budget block
  → close).
  - §8 Build plan — phased, with an explicit cut order for when time runs short.
  - §9–11 — risks, resume bullets, open questions.

  Two things I deliberately flagged rather than faked

  1. Mogami's exact API (class/method names, Maven coords) isn't on their public
  site — it's in their GitBook. I made nailing it a Phase-0 spike instead of
  inventing method names.
  2. ERC-8004 signatures are grounded in the reference contracts
  (https://github.com/erc-8004/erc-8004-contracts) — real
  getSummary/giveFeedback/register functions, real testnet deployments on
  Sepolia + Polygon Amoy.

  The one decision that gates everything

  Which chain/facilitator the organizers expect. The brief's DeFi idea
  name-drops Avalanche, but Mogami and Coinbase's facilitator are Base-centric,
  and the ERC-8004 reference deployments are on Sepolia/Polygon Amoy. These
  don't necessarily line up — and the answer changes your Phase-0 spikes. I'd
  ask the organizers this before writing any code.

  Want me to scaffold the Spring Boot project (Phase 1 skeleton — proxy, intent
  state machine, policy engine, ledger) so you're ready to drop in the chain
  integration once the spikes land? Or refine any section of the doc first?

⏺ Both facts resolved, and one of them changes the plan materially:

  - Avalanche x402 is a first-class, well-documented path — there's a whole
  Avalanche Builder Hub x402 academy (https://build.avax.network/academy/blockch
  ain/x402-payment-infrastructure/04-x402-on-avalanche/03-facilitators).
  Facilitators with confirmed Fuji support: x402.rs
  (https://build.avax.network/integrations/x402-rs) (open-source) and thirdweb
  x402 (https://build.avax.network/integrations/thirdweb-x402). Settlement uses
  USDC's EIP-3009 transferWithAuthorization — gasless for the client; the
  facilitator's hot wallet sponsors the AVAX gas. Sub-second finality. Test USDC
  comes from Circle's Fuji faucet.
  - Mogami's Fuji support is not confirmed — searches surfaced x402.rs and
  thirdweb on Avalanche, not Mogami. This is the one risk I need to design
  around.

  The implication: the facilitator is a separate process regardless of language,
  so swapping Mogami's facilitator for a Fuji-confirmed one (x402.rs/thirdweb)
  doesn't touch the Java story — our treasury stays Spring Boot. For client-side
  signing, Mogami's SDK is now contingent on whether it's Fuji-configurable;
  the clean fallback is to sign EIP-3009 authorizations directly with web3j
  (which is arguably a stronger resume line). Let me fold all of this in.

⏺ Done. DESIGN.md is now aligned with the submission rules. Here's what changed
  and why it matters:

  What the Avalanche requirement changed

  1. Chain is locked, not open. Removed the "ask the organizers" open question —
  it's Fuji C-Chain (chainId 43113). Good news: C-Chain is EVM, so nothing 
  about the Java/web3j/Solidity plan changes — it ports unchanged.
  2. Facilitator pivot (the important one). Mogami's Fuji support is unverified,
  but the facilitator is a separate process regardless of language, so I
  swapped it for a Fuji-confirmed one — x402.rs (https://facilitator.x402.rs/)
  (open-source, runnable locally) or thirdweb x402
  (https://build.avax.network/integrations/thirdweb-x402). This doesn't touch 
  the Java story — your treasury stays Spring Boot.
  3. Client signing now has a fallback. Mogami's SDK is preferred if it's
  Fuji-configurable; otherwise sign the EIP-3009 transferWithAuthorization
  payload directly with web3j. That fallback is arguably a stronger resume line.
  4. ERC-8004 self-deploy is now confirmed, not "likely." The reference
  registries are on Sepolia/Polygon Amoy, not Fuji — so deploying them to Fuji
  yourself (Foundry/Hardhat) is a planned task and a resume bullet.
  5. New §12 Submission requirements — deployed-on-C-Chain/demo-on-Fuji
  checklist, mainnet as optional bonus, and an explicit note that since you're
  building fresh, the entire prototype is the delta (state this in the
  submission).
  6. Demo + risks updated — gasless-for-payer and sub-second finality as live
  talking points; risk table now reflects the real failure modes (Mogami-Fuji,
  faucet limits, self-deploy).

  The two things that are still genuinely unknown

  These are the Phase-0 spikes — do them before committing build time:
  - Does Mogami's client SDK support Fuji? If no → web3j EIP-3009 path.
  - x402.rs/thirdweb facilitator end-to-end on Fuji — settle one hardcoded USDC
  payment before building anything on top.

  The plan is now buildable as-is. When you're ready, I'd suggest knocking out
  the three Phase-0 spikes first (they de-risk everything downstream), then I
  can scaffold the Phase-1 Spring Boot skeleton — proxy, intent state machine,
  policy engine, ledger — which has zero chain dependencies and can be built in
  parallel with the spikes. Want me to start that scaffold now, or run a spike
  first?

✻ Baked for 2m 25s

⏺ Recon's back, and it resolved the biggest unknown decisively — Mogami does not
  support Avalanche Fuji. It's not a config gap; the supported chains are a
  hardcoded whitelist (Base + Solana only) in x402-commons/Networks.java,
  verified from source. Using it would mean forking an AGPL-3.0 library. So
  Mogami is out, and the "fallback" web3j path becomes the primary — which
  actually simplifies the plan (one fewer dependency, no AGPL) and strengthens
  the resume story (you implement x402 signing yourself).

  The rest came back fully grounded. Let me capture all the verified facts in a
  reference doc, then update the plan.

      149  ### 4.3 Reputation oracle (ERC-8004 integration)
      150  - **web3j** reads against the deployed ERC-8004 registries.
      151  - Identity Registry (ERC-721): resolve payee wallet → `agentId
           ` → registration metadata.
      152 -- Reputation Registry: `getSummary(agentId, clients[], tag1, t
          -ag2) → (count, summaryValue, decimals)`                       
      153 -  gives an aggregate score; normalize to 0–100.               
      152 +- Reputation Registry: `getSummary(agentId, clients[], tag1, t
          +ag2) → (count, summaryValue, decimals)`.                      
      153 +  `summaryValue` is an **average** in fixed-point; real value 
          += `summaryValue / 10^decimals`. The                           
      154 +  contract enforces **no** 0–100 range — it's our convention. 
          +**Decision:** we write feedback as an                         
      155 +  integer **0–100 with `valueDecimals=0`**, so `getSummary` re
          +turns the average score directly;                             
      156 +  `count==0` (unknown agent) → **deny**. Clamp to `[0,100]`. (
          +Full semantics in `SPIKE-FINDINGS.md` §4.)                    
      157  - **Caching:** Caffeine cache, short TTL (e.g. 30s). One RPC r
           ound-trip per payment is too slow and
      158    rate-limited on public testnet RPCs. Cache-freshness-vs-corr
           ectness is a deliberate trade-off —
      159    document it (a stale-but-recent reputation read is acceptabl
           e; a slow one breaks the demo).

⏺ All unknowns are now resolved and both docs are internally consistent. Here's
  what the spikes settled.

  What the recon decided

  The big one — Mogami is out. Verified from its source
  (x402-commons/Networks.java): supported chains are a hardcoded whitelist of
  Base + Solana, with no chainId config surface. Fuji isn't reachable without
  forking an AGPL-3.0 library. So the plan now uses:

  - Facilitator: self-hosted x402.rs (ghcr.io/x402-rs/x402-facilitator) —
  source-confirmed Fuji support (eip155:43113, Fuji USDC baked in). Config +
  HTTP API captured.
  - Client signing: web3j, ourselves — sign EIP-3009 transferWithAuthorization,
  base64 the X-PAYMENT header. This is now the primary path: one fewer
  dependency, no AGPL, and a stronger "I built the x402 client in Java" resume
  line.

  Every concrete value the build needs is now pinned and sourced (in
  SPIKE-FINDINGS.md):
  - Fuji chainId 43113, RPC, USDC 0x5425…Bc65, and — the detail that silently
  breaks EIP-712 — the USDC domain name:"USD Coin"/version:"2", verified by live
  on-chain call, not docs.
  - The exact x402 v1 wire format (402 body, X-PAYMENT, settlement header) and
  EIP-712 type + TYPEHASH, including the v ∈ {27,28} packing gotcha.
  - ERC-8004 exact signatures, Hardhat v3 / Solidity 0.8.24 toolchain, Fuji
  already in the repo config, deploy order (Identity → Reputation), and the
  getSummary semantics → resolved the reputation-normalization question (write
  scores as 0–100, valueDecimals=0; count==0 → deny).
  - x402.rs config.json for Fuji, Docker run command, /verify + /settle schemas.

  The two docs now

  - DESIGN.md — the plan, updated end-to-end (architecture diagram, flow, stack,
  risks, phases, open questions all reflect the resolved choices).
  - SPIKE-FINDINGS.md — the verified build reference (addresses, ABIs, wire
  formats, config, plus a §6 live-execution checklist).

  What genuinely still needs you (and only this)

  The recon hit its ceiling at anything requiring a funded wallet — I can't
  clear captcha faucets or broadcast txs headless. Those steps (in
  SPIKE-FINDINGS.md §6) are mechanical: fund two wallets (AVAX + USDC), deploy
  ERC-8004, run x402.rs, smoke-test one payment through /verify → /settle.

  But I can write everything you'll run — the ERC-8004 deploy script, the Fuji
  config.json, the web3j EIP-3009 signer, and a standalone smoke-test harness —
  so your manual part is just pasting a private key and clicking faucets. That's
  the natural next move: it de-risks the single highest-risk detail (signature
  validity) before any treasury code exists, and it's all buildable now without
  waiting on the faucets.

  Want me to generate that Phase-0 smoke-test bundle next, or scaffold the
  Phase-1 Spring Boot skeleton (proxy + intent state machine + policy engine +
  ledger, zero chain dependencies) so both tracks move in parallel?

⏺ Two config problems surfaced, one of them mine:

  1. My .env.example has inline comments after values (e.g.
  NETWORK=avalanche-fuji  # ...). source strips those, but docker --env-file
  does not — it would pass the comment as part of the value and corrupt the run.
  That's a bug in my template.
  2. PAY_TO is invalid — 0x1f04…e129d is 64 hex chars (32 bytes), but an
  Ethereum address is 20 bytes (42 chars). It can't work as a recipient. (If
  that was a private key pasted by mistake, consider it exposed — rotate it.)

  Let me fix the template (clean comments), then repair the .env on Linux. For
  PAY_TO, the destination is arbitrary for a smoke test and I have a valid
  address you control — the facilitator wallet 0x6f40…fbf (from the startup log)
  — so I'll point it there; easy to change later. First, the template fix on
  the Mac:

⏺ The space in USDC_DOMAIN_NAME=USD Coin exposes a real conflict: source needs
  the value quoted (else bash runs Coin as a command), but docker --env-file
  needs it unquoted (else the quotes become literal). My smoke.sh does both —
  sources and --env-files — so it can't win.

  The fix: since the runtime is Docker-only, standardize on unquoted values 
  consumed via --env-file, never sourced. smoke.sh only needs FACILITATOR_URL
  for the health check, which I can grep out instead of sourcing. Let me correct
  the script and the docs.

  removed.

  Where we landed

  The hard part is done. Per the plan, the single highest-risk detail was
  whether the EIP-712 signature would be valid — a wrong domain or v-byte fails
  silently. It's now proven correct against the live facilitator: it parsed our
  payload, accepted the signature, and only stopped at the balance check.
  Domain, v-packing, network string, envelope, field names — all confirmed.

  Along the way I also closed the dev loop and fixed several real cross-machine
  issues:

⏺ Let me lay out the stages, then start building Stage 1 and test it on your
  laptop as I go. (Noted on the laptop — I'll keep everything scoped to the
  project dir and named containers, no system changes, no destructive commands;
  builds stay in Docker as we set up.)

  Implementation stages

  The key architectural idea: interfaces isolate the chain, so Phase 1 is fully
  functional and testable with stubs, then Phase 2 swaps in real implementations
  without touching the core.

  1. Stage 1 — Skeleton + policy engine (no DB, no chain): Spring Boot project,
  domain model (PaymentIntent states, denial reasons), and the policy engine as 
  a pure function (per-tx cap, daily budget, allowlist, velocity, reputation
  tiers) with exhaustive unit tests. This is the centerpiece and runs instantly.
  2. Stage 2 — Persistence + ledger + state machine: Postgres (Docker) + Flyway,
  repositories, the PaymentIntent lifecycle with idempotency, and the
  double-entry ledger + daily-budget query. Integration-tested against real
  Postgres.
  3. Stage 3 — Proxy + orchestration: POST /proxy with X-Agent-Key auth wiring
  it all together, behind two interfaces — ReputationProvider and
  PaymentExecutor — with stub implementations. Full flow tested over HTTP.
  4. Stage 4 — Chain integration (Phase 2): port Eip3009Signer, real facilitator
  settle, ERC-8004 reputation oracle + feedback, reconciliation job. Swaps the
  stubs for real impls.

  Starting Stage 1 now.

  over real HTTP. The architecture choice that paid off: the two stub interfaces
  are clean seams — Phase 2 swaps in real implementations without touching the
  orchestration or its tests.

  The whole loop worked smoothly throughout: I wrote and committed on the Mac,
  and built/tested/ran on your laptop via SSH in Docker containers (no
  Java/Maven/Node installed there, nothing touched on the system — treasury-pg
  and the maven build image are the only footprint, and the app container is
  already cleaned up).

  What's next (Phase 2 — chain integration)

  The seams to fill, all building on what's already proven:
  1. Real PaymentExecutor — port the smoke-test's Eip3009Signer so settlement is
  an actual Fuji tx (the signing path is already proven on-chain).
  2. ERC-8004 — deploy the registries to Fuji (contracts/), then a web3j
  ReputationProvider reading getSummary + an async feedback writer.
  3. Reconciliation @Scheduled job.

  treasury-pg is left running on your laptop for fast iteration
  (./scripts/dev-db.sh down stops it).

  Want me to start Phase 2 — beginning with the real PaymentExecutor
  (highest-value: it turns the demo's settlements into real on-chain
  transactions), or with the ERC-8004 deploy first so reputation becomes real
  too? I'd suggest the PaymentExecutor first since the signing is already
  validated, but either order works.

⏺ Done and pushed (7ae3d99).

  Phase 2a complete — real settlement is live

  The PaymentExecutor is no longer a stub. The treasury now signs EIP-3009
  authorizations and settles real USDC on Avalanche Fuji, end-to-end through
  /proxy:

  ┌─────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │          Path           │                    Result                     │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Agent pays good         │ 200 SETTLED, real tx 0x762b0fe1… — receipt    │
  │ merchant (0.05 USDC)    │ status 0x1, block 56,239,960                  │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Agent pays sketchy      │ 402 DENIED (reputation) — no chain call,      │
  │ merchant                │ policy short-circuits                         │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Offline test suite      │ 31 tests green (stub stays default)           │
  └─────────────────────────┴───────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  The design held up nicely: flipping x402.enabled=true swapped the stub for the
  real executor with zero changes to the orchestrator or tests, and the
  smoke-test signer ported in unchanged because it was already proven on-chain.
  The guardrail-before-settlement ordering is the key correctness property, and
  it's confirmed live — a low-reputation counterparty never reaches the signing
  path.

  Remaining Phase 2 work

  Reputation is still the in-memory stub, so the next increment makes that real
  too:

  1. Deploy ERC-8004 registries to Fuji (contracts/) + seed the two demo
  merchants on-chain.
  2. Real ReputationProvider — web3j read of getSummary, Caffeine-cached,
  @Primary to supersede the stub.
  3. Async feedback writer — giveFeedback after settlement (closes the
  reputation loop, great demo beat: a merchant's score ticking up live).
  4. Reconciliation @Scheduled job.
  5. Minor: move the executor's network call outside the DB transaction in
  TreasuryService.

  One note I'd flag for honesty: each live settlement spends real testnet USDC
  from the treasury wallet (40 USDC balance, 0.05–0.10 per run), so it's fine
  for many demo runs but not infinite — the faucet tops it up if needed.

  Want me to continue with the ERC-8004 deploy next? That's the bigger lift
  (Hardhat deploy to Fuji, then the web3j reader), but it's what turns the
  reputation gate from seeded numbers into genuinely on-chain trust — and it
  sets up the most compelling part of the demo.

✻ Worked for 5m 47s

❯ continue with the ERC-8004 deploy                                             

⏺ Now ERC-8004. Quick decision on approach, since it shapes everything: the
  reference repo is Hardhat v3 + UUPS proxies + a CREATE2 "vanity" deploy that
  needs a pre-funded factory — a lot of fragile machinery for a hackathon. The
  integration we actually need is narrow: read a counterparty's reputation
  (getSummary) and write feedback (giveFeedback).

  So I'll deploy lean ERC-8004-compatible registries — same verified function
  signatures (register, giveFeedback, getSummary), but minimal and fully under
  our control — via a small Hardhat 2.x project run in a Node container (the box
  has only Docker). This is predictable, fast, and I'll document it honestly as
  a compatible subset rather than the full upgradeable reference impl.

  Let me write the contracts and deploy tooling.

  This is the hackathon's headline goal — an agent moving real value with x402
  settlement gated by ERC-8004 reputation, autonomously. The flag-gated design
  kept all 31 tests offline and deterministic while the same code runs fully
  on-chain when enabled.

  A couple of honest notes:
  - These are lean ERC-8004-compatible registries (same
  register/giveFeedback/getSummary signatures), not the full UUPS reference impl
  — a deliberate, documented trade-off for sprint reliability. The treasury's
  integration is real either way.
  - Each enabled run spends real testnet USDC + AVAX from the
  treasury/facilitator wallets; balances are ample for demo runs,
  faucet-toppable if needed.

  What's left for a complete demo

  From the plan, in priority order:
  1. Async feedback writer — giveFeedback after each settlement. This closes the
  reputation loop and is a great live demo beat (a merchant's score visibly
  ticking up as the agent transacts).
  2. Reconciliation @Scheduled job — re-verify settled/failed intents against
  chain (defense-in-depth; strong resume/interview material).
  3. Thin dashboard — budget burn-down + payment feed + blocked-with-reason (the
  visual that sells the demo).
  4. Minor cleanup — move the executor's network call outside the DB
  transaction.

  I'd suggest the feedback writer next (completes the ERC-8004 story and gives
  the demo its most compelling moment), then the dashboard. Want me to continue
  with the feedback writer?

❯ continue with the feedback writer                                             

⏺ The feedback writer closes the reputation loop: after a successful settlement,
  the treasury writes giveFeedback to the ERC-8004 registry, so an agent's
  payments build the merchant's on-chain reputation. It must be async (it's a
  signed tx, off the request path) and follow the same stub/real-gated pattern.

  First, the interface and the no-op default:

⏺ Done and pushed (0461e4d).

  Feedback writer complete — the core loop is whole

  The full agentic-payments cycle now runs autonomously and entirely on-chain:

  pay (x402) → settle → write feedback (ERC-8004) → reputation updates → gates 
  the next payment

  Live-verified: paying good-data-co twice raised its on-chain reputation 85 → 
  90 via two real feedback transactions, while sketchy-data-inc (never paid)
  stayed at 12. 33 offline tests green, the chain paths gated behind flags so
  tests stay deterministic.

  That satisfies the hackathon's headline goal and its bonus — payments that are
  identity- and reputation-aware, combining both protocols, no human in the
  loop. Everything you need to demo the thesis exists and works.

  A couple of honest notes from the run:
  - The two feedback writes used the 6-second gap I inserted to avoid a nonce 
  collision (both async writes sign from the same treasury wallet). Under
  rapid-fire concurrent payments that's a real edge — fine for the demo, but I'd
  add proper nonce sequencing before calling it production-grade. Noted in the
  plan.
  - Each enabled run spends real testnet USDC + AVAX; balances remain healthy,
  faucet-toppable.

  What's left (all polish, not core)

  The thesis is proven; the rest makes it land:
  1. Thin dashboard — budget burn-down, live payment feed, blocked-with-reason.
  Highest demo value: it's the visual that sells the guardrail story to judges
  (right now everything's curl/logs).
  2. Reconciliation @Scheduled job — re-verify settled/failed intents vs chain.
  Strong "we don't trust our own ledger" interview material.
  3. Cleanups — executor network call outside the DB transaction;
  feedback-writer nonce sequencing.

  My recommendation: the dashboard next. The backend is fully demoable but
  invisible; a simple UI turns "trust me, the logs show it" into a screen where
  a judge watches a payment settle and a blocked one flash red with its reason.
  Want me to build it?

⏺ Found it — and it's not a dashboard bug. The sketchy call returned 401 
  Unauthorized, not 402. Root cause: tests and the live app share the same
  Postgres database, and a test left an agent-1 row with a dummy API-key hash
  ("hash"). The DataSeeder only seeds if absent, so it skipped the stale row —
  and demo-key-agent-1 no longer authenticated. (In the earlier run, the one
  "settled" row was from a moment the correct hash happened to be present; the
  denials 401'd and rolled back.)

  Two proper fixes: isolate tests onto their own database so they can't pollute
  runtime, and make the seeder upsert the demo agent so it's always correct. Let
  me do both.

⏺ Dashboard built, verified, and pushed (c993cda).

  Dashboard done

  A self-contained live dashboard at http://localhost:8090/ (no frontend
  framework, no extra deps):
  - Budget burn-down bar per agent (spent / daily, turns red when exceeded) +
  caps/velocity/min-reputation
  - Live payment feed polling every 2s — color-coded: SETTLED green with a
  Snowtrace link, DENIED red with the reason inline (the money shot), FAILED
  amber
  - Connection status indicator

  Verified end-to-end: the feed correctly shows a settled payment plus both
  denials (PER_TX_CAP_EXCEEDED, REPUTATION_BELOW_THRESHOLD) and the agent's
  burn-down. 35 tests green.

  A real bug caught and fixed along the way

  The first run only showed 1 of 3 payments. It turned out not to be a dashboard
  bug — it was test/runtime database pollution: tests and the live app shared
  one Postgres DB, a test had left an agent-1 row with a dummy key-hash, and the
  create-if-absent seeder skipped it, so demo-key-agent-1 started returning 401
  and the denials rolled back. Fixed properly:
  - Tests now run on a separate treasury_test database — they can never pollute
  runtime data again.
  - DataSeeder upserts the demo agent, so its key/policy are always correct on
  boot.

  This is exactly the kind of thing that "test as you build, verify the real
  app" surfaces that unit tests alone wouldn't — worth a mention in interviews.

  State of the build

  The whole thing is demo-ready. One command path:
  ./scripts/dev-db.sh up
  # run app: defaults = offline/stub, or -e X402_ENABLED=true -e
  ERC8004_ENABLED=true for real chain
  open http://localhost:8090/
  You get the visual guardrail story (settle / reputation-block / budget-block)
  backed by real x402 settlement and real ERC-8004 reputation when the flags are
  on.

  What's left (hardening, not core)

  1. Reconciliation @Scheduled job — re-verify settled/failed intents against
  chain (defense-in-depth; strong interview material).
  2. Cleanups — move the executor's network call outside the DB transaction;
  sequence the feedback-writer nonce (the concurrent-write edge I flagged
  earlier).

  These are polish — the core thesis and the demo are complete. Want me to do
  the reconciliation job next, or pause here? Given you're targeting a hackathon
  submission, it might also be a good moment to put together the demo
  script/README walkthrough and the resume bullets — I can do that too.

  Part 1 — Reconciliation job

  The valuable check here is defense-in-depth: don't blindly trust our own
  ledger — periodically re-verify that intents we marked SETTLED actually have a
  confirmed transaction on-chain, and flag any mismatch. I'll make the core
  logic pure/testable (a ReceiptStatusProvider seam) so it unit-tests without a
  chain.

      325 -vs. correctness on risk reads, reconciliation as defense-in-de
          -pth.                                                          
      318 +- Built a **policy-enforcement payment gateway** in **Java 21 
          +/ Spring Boot** that lets autonomous AI                       
      319 +  agents transact under per-tx, daily-budget, velocity, and co
          +unterparty-reputation constraints —                           
      320 +  **agents hold spending authority, never private keys** (the 
          +treasury custodies keys and signs only                        
      321 +  after policy approval).                                     
      322 +- Implemented an **idempotent payment-intent state machine** +
          + **double-entry ledger** (Postgres,                           
      323 +  Flyway) with a **scheduled on-chain reconciliation** job tha
          +t re-verifies settlements against chain                       
      324 +  receipts — defense-in-depth against ledger/chain drift.     
      325 +- Integrated the **x402** protocol end-to-end: signed **EIP-30
          +09 (`transferWithAuthorization`)**                            
      326 +  authorizations with web3j and settled real USDC via an x402 
          +facilitator (gasless for the payer).                          
      327 +- Integrated **ERC-8004** on-chain identity + reputation as a 
          +real-time risk signal (web3j reads,                           
      328 +  Caffeine-cached) and **closed the trust loop** by writing `g
          +iveFeedback` after each payment, so a                         
      329 +  counterparty's on-chain reputation reflects its transaction 
          +history.                                                      
      330 +- Designed the system so the chain layer sits behind interface
          +s (`PaymentExecutor`,                                         
      331 +  `ReputationProvider`, `FeedbackWriter`), keeping a **35-test
          + suite** fully offline/deterministic                          
      332 +  while the same code runs live on-chain via config flags.    
      333  
      334 +Interview stories it generates: exactly-once payment semantics
          + (idempotency key + unique constraint);                       
      335 +custody separation (authority vs. keys); EIP-712 signing pitfa
          +lls (domain/`v` make-or-break);                               
      336 +cache-freshness vs. correctness on risk reads; reconciliation

  Reconciliation job — live-verified

  The scheduled job runs and audits real settlements against the chain:

  reconciliation: 0 settled checked, 0 mismatched     ← first tick (before
  payment)
  reconciliation: 1 settled checked, 0 mismatched     ← after a real payment
  settled
  reconciliation: 1 settled checked, 0 mismatched

  It found the SETTLED intent (real tx 0x7c08a69a…), pulled its receipt from
  Fuji, confirmed status 0x1, and reported 0 mismatches — flagging a WARN if our
  ledger ever disagreed with the chain. The core logic is behind a
  ReceiptStatusProvider seam so it's unit-tested without a chain (3 tests; 38 
  total green), and @EnableScheduling only does real work when
  x402.enabled=true.

  Demo + resume material

  - DEMO.md — start-the-stack commands plus the 4-beat live script: real payment
  → reputation block → budget block → reputation rising as the merchant earns
  it. Includes reset and offline-fallback notes.
  - DESIGN.md §10 — refreshed résumé bullets matching what's actually built and
  verified (x402 EIP-3009 settlement, ERC-8004 read + feedback loop, idempotent
  ledger, scheduled reconciliation, interface-gated 38-test suite), plus
  interview stories — including the real test/runtime DB-pollution bug and its
  fix.

❯ Alright. Now just explain to me like a layman how I can run this application  
  and demo it. Assume I will run it directly on my linux laptop. Do I need a    
  real agent running? How do I configure, how do I run, how do I check logs and 
  monitor, step by step                                                         

⏺ Great question — let me strip out all the jargon and walk you through it as if
  you're sitting at the Linux laptop right now.

  First: do you need a real AI agent? No.

  The "agent" is just anything that sends an HTTP request to the treasury. For
  the demo, you are the agent — you'll send those requests yourself with a
  one-line curl command (a terminal tool for making web requests). There's no
  LLM, no bot, nothing to install. You type a command that means "pay this
  merchant $0.10," and the treasury does its thing.

  The mental model — what actually runs

  Think of it as 3 programs running on your laptop (each in its own Docker
  container, so you don't install anything), plus the blockchain out on the 
  internet:

  ┌──────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────┬──────────────────┐
  │    Piece     │             Plain English             │  Container name  │
  ├──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ Database     │ Remembers every payment + budgets     │ treasury-pg      │
  ├──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ Facilitator  │ The "card machine" that actually      │ x402-facilitator │
  │              │ pushes USDC onto the blockchain       │                  │
  ├──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ Treasury app │ The brain — checks rules, decides     │ treasury-app     │
  │              │ yes/no, signs payments                │                  │
  ├──────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ Avalanche    │ The test blockchain (free             │ (internet)       │
  │ Fuji         │ play-money), already out there        │                  │
  └──────────────┴───────────────────────────────────────┴──────────────────┘

  Docker is the only thing your laptop needs installed — everything else runs
  inside Docker.

  Two ways to run it

  - Offline mode (practice): fake payments, no blockchain, no real money,
  nothing can fail. Great for rehearsing. Only needs the database + app (2
  containers).
  - Real mode (the actual demo): real USDC moves on the test blockchain, real
  transaction links you can show. Needs all 3 containers + your funded wallets
  (which you already set up).

  My advice: practice in offline mode once, then do the real demo in real mode.

  ---
  Step by step

  Step 0 — open a terminal and go to the project

  cd ~/Documents/agent-treasury
  git pull        # get the latest

  Step 1 — configuration (you've basically already done this)

  All settings live in one file called .env. You already created it earlier
  (funded wallet keys, the deployed contract addresses, etc.), so there's 
  nothing new to configure. If you ever want to peek at the non-secret settings:
  grep -v PRIVATE_KEY .env
  (That shows everything except your secret keys.)

  Step 2 — start the database (needed in both modes)

  ./scripts/dev-db.sh up
  You'll see postgres ready. That's the memory.

  ---
  Step 3 — start the app

  Option A — Offline mode (practice, no blockchain):
  docker run -d --name treasury-app --network host --env-file .env \
    -v "$PWD/treasury":/app -w /app -v "$HOME/.m2":/root/.m2 \
    maven:3.9-eclipse-temurin-21 mvn -q spring-boot:run

  Option B — Real mode (the demo, real blockchain): first start the facilitator,
  then the app with two extra switches turned on:
  # the "card machine"
  docker run -d --name x402-facilitator --restart unless-stopped \
    -v "$PWD/infra/facilitator/config.json:/app/config.json:ro" \
    --env-file .env -p 8080:8080 ghcr.io/x402-rs/x402-facilitator

  # the brain, with real-chain switches ON
  docker run -d --name treasury-app --network host --env-file .env \
    -e X402_ENABLED=true -e ERC8004_ENABLED=true \
    -v "$PWD/treasury":/app -w /app -v "$HOME/.m2":/root/.m2 \
    maven:3.9-eclipse-temurin-21 mvn -q spring-boot:run

  The only difference between the two modes is those two -e ...=true lines and
  the facilitator. That's it.

  Step 4 — wait for it to wake up (~30 seconds)

  The app takes about half a minute to boot. Check it's ready:
  docker logs treasury-app | tail -20
  When you see a line like Started TreasuryApplication, it's up. (In real mode
  you'll also see two lines confirming reputation enabled and settlement enabled
  — that's how you know the blockchain switches took effect.)

  ---
  How to watch it / monitor (this is what you show on screen)

  The dashboard — your main screen

  Open a browser on the laptop:
  http://localhost:8090/
  You'll see each agent's budget bar (how much it's spent today) and a live 
  payment feed that updates every 2 seconds — green for settled payments (with a
  clickable blockchain link), red for blocked ones with the reason written
  right there. This is the thing to have on screen during the demo.

  The logs — the play-by-play

  In a separate terminal, watch the app narrate what it's doing:
  docker logs -f treasury-app          # -f = follow live; Ctrl+C to stop 
  watching
  And the facilitator (real mode), if you want to see settlements:
  docker logs -f x402-facilitator

  Are my containers running?

  docker ps        # lists running containers; you want to see treasury-app, 
  treasury-pg, (and x402-facilitator in real mode)

  ---
  Driving the demo (you = the agent)

  Paste this once to set up shortcuts (so you can type pay instead of a giant
  command):
  USDC=0x5425890298aed601595a70AB815c96711a31Bc65
  GOOD=0x6f409644a8a0b598284e8ca1a7562759f2189fbf
  SKETCHY=0x000000000000000000000000000000000000dEaD
  pay(){ curl -s -X POST localhost:8090/proxy -H "X-Agent-Key: demo-key-agent-1"
  \
    -H "Content-Type: application/json" -d
  "{\"payee\":\"$1\",\"asset\":\"$USDC\",\"amountAtomic\":$2}"; echo; }
  (amountAtomic is just USDC in millionths — 100000 = $0.10.)

  Now the four demo moments — run each, and point at the dashboard as it reacts:

  pay $GOOD 100000      # 1. PAYS — green row, real blockchain link
  pay $SKETCHY 100000   # 2. BLOCKED — red row, "REPUTATION_BELOW_THRESHOLD" 
  (bad merchant)
  pay $GOOD 600000      # 3. BLOCKED — "PER_TX_CAP_EXCEEDED" ($0.60 > $0.50 
  limit)
  pay $GOOD 100000      # pays again, building the good merchant's reputation

  Each command prints the result right in the terminal too (e.g.
  "state":"SETTLED" with a txHash). In real mode, copy that txHash into
  https://testnet.snowtrace.io/ to show the real on-chain transaction.

  ▎ The full presenter script with timing and what-to-say is in your private 
  ▎ DEMO-GUIDE.private.md (only on this laptop, never uploaded). DEMO.md is the 
  ▎ shareable version.

  ---
  When you're done — stop / reset

  docker rm -f treasury-app x402-facilitator      # stop the app + facilitator
  ./scripts/dev-db.sh down                         # stop the database 
  (optional)

  To wipe the payment history clean (e.g. between rehearsals) but keep
  everything running:
  docker exec treasury-pg psql -U treasury -d treasury -c \
    "truncate agents, payment_intent, journal_entry cascade;"
  The app automatically re-creates the demo agent the next time it starts, so a
  clean slate is one restart away.

  ---
  The 30-second version

  1. cd ~/Documents/agent-treasury
  2. ./scripts/dev-db.sh up
  3. Start the facilitator + app (real mode commands above)
  4. Wait 30s, open http://localhost:8090/
  5. Paste the pay shortcut, then run the four pay … commands
  6. Watch the dashboard react; click a green link to show the real blockchain
  transaction
  7. docker rm -f treasury-app x402-facilitator when done

  One thing worth doing before the real demo: run the quick wallet balance check
  from your private guide (or just do a test pay $GOOD 50000 an hour before) —
  the #1 thing that breaks a live blockchain demo is a wallet running low on gas
  or the free faucet being slow. If anything misbehaves on the day, fall back
  to offline mode (drop the two -e ...=true switches) — the guardrail story
  still demos perfectly, just without the real transaction links.

  Want me to verify your laptop is currently in a ready state (containers, .env,
  wallet balances) so there are no surprises, or is this enough to go on?

✻ Worked for 1m 28s

❯ I have one doubt regarding the working of x402. Say a merchant responds with  
  402 Payment Required, and the agent pays through the treasury... Now, does    
  the merchant check which wallet it is coming from? Because that wallet        
  belongs to the treasury, not the agent, right? So what is stopping an agent   
  from just picking another transaction on the chain with the same amount and   
  the same destination address and claiming it as its own payment? Please       
  consider me a noob to x402 settlements, and explain the flow to me            

⏺ Excellent question — this gets at the heart of why x402 is secure, and the
  answer clears up a misconception that's very natural to have. Let me build it
  up from scratch.

  The misconception to clear first

  You're picturing this flow:

  ▎ Agent sends money on-chain → tells merchant "I paid! Here's transaction 
  ▎ 0xabc..., go check the blockchain" → merchant looks it up and grants access.

  That is exactly the broken design, and x402 does NOT work this way. If it did,
  your attack would work perfectly — you could point at any matching
  transaction and claim it. "Show me a receipt" is forgeable; that's the whole
  problem with naive crypto payments.

  x402 flips it around: the agent never sends money and then proves it. The 
  agent hands the merchant a signed permission slip, and the merchant pulls the 
  money itself.

  The analogy: a one-time signed cheque

  Think of the payment as a cheque you hand the merchant:

  - It's made out to that specific merchant (their address).
  - It's for an exact amount.
  - It has a unique cheque number that can only be cashed once.
  - It expires in a few minutes.
  - It's signed by your bank account (your wallet's private key).

  The merchant doesn't take your word that you paid — they deposit the cheque 
  themselves. That single act is the payment. There's nothing to "claim" and
  nothing to look up.

  In x402 terms, that "cheque" is an EIP-3009 signed authorization
  (transferWithAuthorization). The signature isn't a transaction — it's
  permission to make exactly one transaction.

  The actual flow, step by step

  1. Merchant: "402 — pay me." Returns the terms: pay 0.10 USDC to address M, on
  Avalanche, within 5 minutes.
  2. Treasury signs an authorization (no money moves yet). It's a message that
  says, literally:
  ▎ "I authorize transferring 0.10 USDC from wallet T (treasury) to address M 
  (merchant), valid until 12:05, with random nonce 0x9f3a...."

  2. This is signed with the treasury's private key. The signature
  mathematically locks in all of those fields — the from, the to, the amount,
  the expiry, and the nonce.
  3. Treasury sends that signature to the merchant (in an HTTP header). Still no
  money moved — it's just the signed cheque.
  4. The merchant (via the facilitator) cashes it: it submits the signature to
  the USDC smart contract on-chain, calling transferWithAuthorization(...). The
  USDC contract itself checks:
    - Is this signature really from wallet T? ✅ (else reject)
    - Has nonce 0x9f3a... been used before? ✅ never → mark it used
    - Is it within the time window? ✅
    - Then, and only then, it moves 0.10 USDC from T to M.

  The merchant grants access because its own settlement succeeded — not because
  the agent claimed anything.

  Now your specific worries, directly

  "Does the merchant check which wallet it's from?"
  It doesn't need to trust a claim about the wallet — the USDC contract 
  cryptographically verifies the authorization was signed by the from wallet
  (the treasury). An invalid or forged signature is simply rejected on-chain. So
  "comes from the treasury wallet" is proven by math, not asserted.

  "What stops an agent from grabbing another transaction with the same amount + 
  destination and claiming it as its own?"
  Several things, layered:

  1. There's no "claim a transaction" move at all. The merchant never accepts a
  tx hash as proof — it settles your signature itself. Pointing at someone
  else's past transaction does nothing, because the protocol never asks "did a
  transaction happen?" It asks "is this a valid signed authorization?" and then
  creates the transaction.
  2. The nonce makes it one-time. Every authorization carries a unique random
  nonce, and the USDC contract permanently records used nonces. Replaying the
  same signed cheque a second time → rejected ("authorization already used").
  You can't reuse even your own legitimate payment, let alone someone else's.
  3. The signature is welded to the exact payment. It covers from + to + amount 
  + time + nonce + the specific USDC contract + chain. You can't take a
  signature made for Merchant M / $0.10 and reuse it for Merchant N or a
  different amount — change any field and the signature no longer matches. And
  you can't fabricate a treasury-signed cheque without the treasury's private
  key.
  4. It expires. The validBefore window (minutes) shrinks the time anything
  could even be attempted.

  So the "proof" the agent provides isn't "a transaction exists" — it's "a 
  cryptographic authorization, signed by the funding wallet, locked to this one 
  merchant and amount, usable exactly once, expiring soon." That's unforgeable
  and unreplayable by construction.

  Tying it back to your system (treasury wallet vs agent)

  You're right that on-chain the payment comes from the treasury's wallet, not
  the agent's — that's the deliberate custody-separation design. The "who is
  who" is answered at two different layers:

  - Is this a valid payment? → answered on-chain by the signature + nonce (it's
  a real, authorized, one-time transfer from the treasury). The merchant cares
  about this.
  - Which agent is this payment for? → answered inside the treasury, before
  signing: the agent authenticated with its API key, the treasury checked its
  budget/policy, and the treasury attributes the payment to that agent in its
  own ledger. The merchant doesn't need to know which agent — but if it wants to
  know it's dealing with a known, reputable counterparty, that's the separate
  job of ERC-8004 identity/reputation (the treasury can be a registered,
  reputation-bearing on-chain identity).

  So: the merchant trusts the payment because it cashed a cryptographically
  valid, one-time cheque; it can additionally trust the counterparty via
  ERC-8004. Two layers, two protocols — which is exactly why the hackathon pairs
  x402 (payment) with ERC-8004 (identity/reputation).

  One small note about our demo specifically: since we control both ends, our
  treasury constructs the requirements and calls the facilitator's /settle
  directly — but the security properties are identical, because settlement still
  goes through the signed-authorization + nonce mechanism on the real USDC
  contract (that's why our reconciliation job can later look up the resulting tx
  hash and confirm it actually landed).

  Want me to point you to where this lives in the code (the Eip3009Signer that
  builds the signature, and the exact fields it signs), so you can see the
  "cheque" being created?

✻ Sautéed for 1m 30s
